[1] First Coffin: The Professor's Study Mystery
[2] Chapter 1: Threat
[3] When discussing the murder of Professor Grieco and the subsequent, equally inexplicable case on Carlyle Street, no amount of bizarre adjectives would suffice. Dr. Fare's friends, who are fond of impossible crimes, would have difficulty finding a more enigmatic or terrifying case in his files. The methods of the murders suggest that the killer was not only invisible and ethereal, but also lighter than air. Evidence indicates that the killer vanished into thin air after killing the first victim, and that he killed the second victim in the middle of a deserted street, despite the presence of people at both ends of the street and no footprints in the snow.
[4] Of course, Inspector Hardly never believed in fairies and magic. His point of view is correct, unless you believe in magic - but the time will come when this story will reveal the true nature of magic. However, some people are beginning to wonder if the shadowy figure that lurked throughout the case was nothing more than an empty shell? Under the fedora, black coat, and childish mask, is there really nothing there, as H.G. Wells depicted in his famous science fiction novel?
[5] Please note the phrase "evidence shows" in the text. When we are not dealing with first-hand direct evidence, we must be cautious. To avoid unnecessary confusion, it is necessary to remind the readers at the beginning of this case which people's testimonies can be trusted one hundred percent. In other words, we must take "someone says the truth" as a premise - otherwise, the logicality of the detective novel would cease to exist, and this story would not need to be told.
[6] Therefore, it is hereby declared that Mr. Stewart Mills did not lie in the Grieco Professor case. His testimony was neither exaggerated nor reduced, but accurately recounted what he saw and heard before and after the event. Similarly, the testimonies of the three unrelated witnesses in the Carlyle Street case (Mr. Short, Mr. Blackwell, and Constable Visher) were completely consistent with the facts.
[7] Based on the above premise, we must first carefully review an incident that led to subsequent murders. This incident is a turning point, a fuse, a challenge. Dr. Fare's notes on this incident are very accurate, including all the important details that Mr. Stewart Mills later reported to him and Inspector Hardly. This event occurred three days before the incident, on Tuesday, February 6th; the location was a private room in the Worcester Bar on Museum Street.
[8] Charles Vernet Grieco, PhD, has lived in England for nearly thirty years and speaks with a perfect British accent. Apart from his occasional rough behavior when emotional, and his preference for old-fashioned top hats and black bow ties, he is more British than his British friends. Grieco's early life is largely unknown. Although he lived a comfortable life, he was very dedicated to his work and made a good income from it. He had been a teacher and a well-known orator and writer. However, in recent years, he has given up these positions and devoted himself to an undefined position at the British Museum, purely voluntary, but with the opportunity to access some manuscripts he calls "low-level magic." Grieco Professor was fascinated by these "low-level magic" and could nod and laugh like a child at any supernatural folklore from vampires to black masses - and it was precisely this magic that tore a bullet through his lungs.
[9] Grieco was a rational man with a playful spark in his eyes. He spoke quickly and had a rough voice that seemed to come from deep in his throat. He had the habit of gnashing his teeth and chuckling softly. He was of medium height, but his chest was thick and muscular, and he was full of vitality. His black beard was trimmed into neat gray stubble. He wore a framed pair of glasses. He walked with a straight back and short, quick steps, occasionally tipping his hat or raising his umbrella in greeting. All of this was familiar to the residents of the area around the museum.
[10] Grieco actually lived in a solid old house at the corner of Russell Square West. He had his daughter Rosette, housekeeper Mrs. Dumont, secretary Stewart Mills, and retired teacher DeRiemer, who was responsible for managing the household library. Grieco provided room and board.
[11] But if you want to find the few close friends of Grimm, you still have to go to the Worcester Bar on Museum Street. They organize a club there, with informal gatherings four or five nights a week in the comfortable booths reserved for the club. Although it's not exactly their private room, the other guests in the bar are rarely mistaken for them; even if someone accidentally walks in, they are welcomed and treated with hospitality. The regulars at the club include the connoisseur of ghost stories, the finicky little bald-headed Petit, the journalist Manguin, the artist Bernabé, but Professor Grimm is undoubtedly the core figure.
[12] He was the master of the gatherings. Almost every night (except for Saturdays and Sundays reserved for work), he would go to the Waverley bar with Stuart Mills, sit in his favorite wingback chair by the crackling fire, sip a steaming rum and water, and launch into a monologue without pause, thoroughly enjoying himself. Mills said that the professor's views were brilliant, but Pettigrew or Burbidge would occasionally engage him in heated debate. Although the professor's demeanor was very cordial, his temper was actually quite explosive. When discussing magic or pseudo-magic, the professor's vast knowledge always left everyone captivated and attentive; he had a childlike passion for mysterious events and dramatic arts, often solving all the puzzles in medieval witchcraft stories at the end in a manner reminiscent of a detective novel. Although they were in the gaslit streets of Bloomsbury, these evenings were not lacking in the charm of a country pub. However, on the evening of February 6th, a sudden gust of cold wind blew open the door, bringing with it ominous portents, and this idyllic time came to an end.
[13] Mills stated that the night was gusty, and the air smelled of an imminent snowfall. Besides Greer and himself, only Patton, Mungin, and Bernabi were gathered around the fireplace. Professor Greer was gesturing with his cigar, eloquently expounding on his views about the vampire legends.
[14] "Frankly, I don't understand your attitude towards this issue," Peetis said. "I have only studied novels on this subject, which are nothing but ghost stories that have never happened. But I have a certain belief in the reality of ghosts. As for things that are undoubtedly real - unless we can prove otherwise, we can only call them 'facts.' In this regard, you are the expert. However, you don't believe in these things that you have devoted your life to. It's like Bradshaw [2] writing an article claiming that steam locomotives are nothing but fairy tales, or the editor of the Encyclopedia Britannica stating in the preface that all entries in the book are not credible."
[15] "What of it?" Greer blurted out his distinctive, gruff roar almost without opening his mouth. "Surely that's not immoral?"
[16] "Isn't that too much of a nitpicker?" Mr. Bernabi said.
[17] Mr. Grierson continued to stare into the fire. Mr. Miers recalls that at the time, it was more accurate to say that the professor was sulking with himself rather than being sarcastic as usual. He sat stiffly in the chair, the cigar balanced perfectly in the center of his lips, like a child sucking on a mint lollipop.
[18] "I know too much," he finally said. "The priests of the temple are not necessarily devout. But the point is not that. What really intrigues me is the cause of these superstitions. How did they originate? What factors led people to believe in them? For example, the vampire legend that we just discussed, which spread like wildfire among the Slavic peoples from Hungary to the whole of Europe between 1730 and 1735. How did the Hungarians prove that the dead could crawl out of their coffins, turn into straw or fluff floating in the air, and then take on human form to attack people?"
[19] "Where is the evidence?" Mr. Bernabi asked.
[20] Mr. Grierson exaggeratedly shrugged: "They dug up bodies from the churchyard and found some twisted, bloodstained on the face, hands, and shrouds. That's the evidence... What's this? During the plague, think of the poor people who were buried alive as dead, struggling desperately to escape from their coffins before they really died. Understand, everyone? This is the cause of the superstition. And that's what interests me
[21] "I am equally interested," a new voice answered.
[22] Mills noted that he did not hear the footsteps of this stranger entering the room, but only vaguely felt a gust of air drifting in from the door. They were all startled by this unexpected visitor, as this room rarely saw strangers, let alone someone interrupting a conversation; or perhaps because of the man's harsh, hoarse, and slightly foreign accent, mixed with a touch of mystery and self-satisfaction. In any case, the suddenness of the event made everyone a little uneasy.
[23] The newcomer was unremarkable, Mills thought. He leaned against the fireplace, wearing a tattered black coat with the collar raised; a shabby soft hat with the brim pulled low; and his gloved hands stroking his chin, concealing most of his face, making it difficult for everyone to see his true face. Apart from his tall stature, ragged clothing, and emaciated physique, Mills could not find any other words to describe him. But in his voice, mannerisms, or habitual gestures, there was a hint of foreign flavor that seemed to echo the familiar.
[24] He spoke again, deliberately imitating Greer's stilted scholarly tone for amusement.
[25] "Disturbing your fine conversation, gentlemen, I apologize," he said haughtily. "But I would like to ask the famous Professor Greer a question."
[26] Miers said that no one wanted to stop him at the time, as everyone was very focused; the man had a chilling presence that broke the warmth and comfort of the fireplace. Greymere, who had been sitting still and brooding, about to put his cigar to his lips, with his eyes glittering behind his thin glasses, like a sculpture by Epstein, was now startled and only growled:
[27] "What?"
[28] "It seems you don't believe that someone can crawl out of a coffin," the other man said, slightly moving his glove away from his chin and pointing, "and wander around, come and go unseen, pass through walls effortlessly, and possess a dangerous power from hell?"
[29] "I don't believe it," Greymere snapped back, "do you?"
[30] "Of course. I have such abilities. And that's not all! I have a more powerful brother who is your deadly threat. I don't intend to take your life, but he does. If he ever pays you a visit…"
[31] The conversation reached its climax, and it suddenly stopped like a crackling sound in the fireplace. Young Mungen, who had been a rugby player, jumped up, while the little Pettis looked around nervously.
[32] "Hey, Greer," Pettis said, "this guy's a nut, should we -" He pointed uneasily at the bell, but the stranger interrupted him.
[33] "Before you make a decision, you might want to see Professor Greer's reaction," he said.
[34] Greer looked at him with a deep scorn: "No, no, no! Have you heard? Let him finish his brother and those coffins -"
[35] "Three coffins," the stranger interjected.
[36] "Three coffins," Grimmer echoed, suppressing his seething anger with a calm tone, "By God, you can say it as many times as you want! Now, may I ask for your name, please?"
[37] The stranger extended his left hand from his pocket and placed a dirty card on the table. The plain business card made everyone regain some composure, quickly erasing the previous doubts as a joke, and they must have concluded that the loud-mouthed guest was just a tired and destitute actor hiding a bee in his hat. Because Mills saw the card read "Pierre Frey, Illusionist" on it. The bottom left corner of the card also had "W.C.1, Karloff Street 2B" printed, with a hastily written "Or via the College Theatre" above it. Grimmer burst out laughing, and Pettis swore and rang the bell to summon the waiter.
[38] "So, that's it," Grimmer tapped the card with his thumb, "I knew something like this would happen. So, you're a magician, then?"
[39] "Is it written on the card?"
[40] "Well, well, if I offended your dignity, please forgive me," Grimmer nodded, snorting with laughter through his nostrils, "Do we have the honor of witnessing your magic?"
[41] "It would be an honor," Frey replied unexpectedly.
[42] His movements were swift and unexpected, surprising everyone. It appeared as though he was about to attack, but that was not the case – there was no physical assault. He bowed slightly over the table towards Grimoire, his gloved hands flipping the collar of his coat down, and then it was back to normal. Although in Mills' memory, he seemed to smile. Grimoire remained stoic and motionless, only lifting his chin slightly, his lips curling in a disdainful arc. He continued to tap the card with his thumb, his expression growing more somber.
[43] "Before you leave, I have one last question for this distinguished professor," Frey interjected. "Soon someone will pay you a visit at night. Once my brother and I join forces, I will also be in danger, but I am prepared to take the risk. I'll say it again, someone will come to see you soon. Do you hope it is me – or my brother?"
[44] "Let my brother come," Grimoire suddenly stood up and roared. "Go to hell!"
[45] After Frey left and the door closed, the others finally woke up from their daze, discussing the events. The curtain had fallen on the first act of the events of February 9th, Saturday night. The remaining fleeting clues would only come together when Dr. Phil pieced together the charred fragments on the glass board. On the night of February 9th, snow fell heavily in London's streets and alleys, and the specter took its first fatal step, filling the prophecy of the three coffins with its contents.
[46] * * *
[47][1] H.G. Wells (Herbert George Wells), a British novelist, journalist, politician, sociologist, and historian, had a profound impact on the field of science fiction. His notable works include "The Time Machine" and "The War of the Worlds."
[48][2] George Bradshaw, a British mapmaker and publisher, achieved great success with the publication of Britain's train timetable in 1839, which continued until 1961.
[49][3] Jacob Epstein, a renowned sculptor, was born in the United States and later moved to Britain. His works often challenged taboo subjects and were highly controversial.
[50] Chapter 2: The Doorway
[51]On this evening, the fire in the living room of Dr. Phil's house on Highgate Hill was crackling with laughter. The red-faced doctor sat comfortably in his old armchair, his smile radiating from behind his spectacles with black ribbons. He frequently tapped the carpet with his cane as he laughed, and whenever friends arrived, Dr. Phil always hosted a party - he enjoyed celebrating both big and small events, and tonight was a double celebration.
[52]The first joy was the arrival of his young friends Ted and Dolly Lambert from America. The second joy was the success of his friend Hardy - remember, he had been promoted to the position of Chief Inspector of the London Police Department - in solving the case of the forged documents in Bethnal Green. Ted and Hardy sat on either side of the fireplace, with Dr. Phil in the middle, a steaming jug of punch in front of him. Mrs. Phil, Mrs. Hardy, and Mrs. Lambert were chatting upstairs, while Dr. Phil and Mr. Hardy were downstairs arguing over some issue, which made Ted feel as if he were at home.
[53]Ted leaned lazily in his chair, reminiscing about the past. Across from him, Detective Hardy had a neatly trimmed beard and iron-gray hair, smiling and making sarcastic gestures with his pipe. Dr. Phil waved his spoon, his voice booming.
[54]They seemed to be arguing fiercely about scientific crime, focusing on photography. Lambert remembered similar debates in the past, which had been ridiculed by the Criminal Investigation Department. One time, Dr. Phil was aimlessly looking for a new hobby when his old friend Bishop Maple led him to see the works of Gross, Jericho, and Mitchell, which left him greatly impressed. Thankfully, Dr. Phil no longer immersed himself in scientific theories, but his chemical laboratory still remained on the top floor. Fortunately, whenever he conducted experiments, the instruments would inevitably malfunction, so far, he had not caused any serious damage, except for burning the curtains with a blowtorch. However, according to Dr. Phil, he had made significant improvements to Gross' method of identifying handwriting from ashes.
[55]As Lambert listened to Hardy's mockery, his thoughts wandered. He watched the firelight dance on the walls of the bookshelf and heard the soft tapping of snow against the closed curtains. He smiled contentedly at himself, thinking there could be no more worries in this perfect situation - could there? He moved slightly, staring at the fire. Who could have guessed that, in this moment of perfect comfort, some small things would spring up like a jack-in-the-box, pricking him like a thorn?
[56] A crime case! Of course not. It was all due to Mangen's overactive imagination and exaggeration. However -
[57] "I don't care what Gross says," Hardy slapped the armrest and spoke loudly. "You people always think experts' words are the truth. In most cases, the characters on the ashes usually reveal nothing..."
[58] Lambert cleared his throat calmly. "By the way," he interjected, "have you heard of the phrase 'Three Coffins'?"
[59] As expected, silence fell. Hardy looked at him suspiciously, while Dr. Phil twirled his spoon, seemingly recalling a cigarette brand or a bar, and his eyes lit up.
[60] "Hey," he rubbed his hands together. "Hey, hey, hey! Are you trying to defuse the situation? Are you serious? What coffins?"
[61] "Well," Raffles said, "strictly speaking, it's not a crime case…"
[62] Hardy whistled.
[63] "—but it's a strange story, unless Mung exaggerated it. I'm quite familiar with Boyle's Mannion. He's lived in the other end of town for years, a nice guy, traveled all over the world, and has a Celtic's rich imagination. He paused, picturing Mannion's dark, unkempt, or even reckless good looks. Mannion may be a bit impatient, but he's steady, broad-minded, and has a pleasant, friendly smile. "To make a long story short, he works for the London Evening Standard now, and this morning I ran into him at Haymarket, and without a word, he dragged me into a bar and told me the whole story. Then, when he found out I knew the great Dr. Phil," Raffles said, "—"
[64] "Come on," Hardy glared at him, "spill the beans about the case."
[65] "Heh, heh, heh," Dr. Phil grinned, "don't be a spoilsport, Hardy. It sounds interesting, kid, what happened next?"
[66] "Uh, Mangan seems to be quite infatuated with a Mr. Grymer, either a speaker or a writer, and he's also smitten with Mr. Grymer's daughter, so he's even more respectful towards the old man. Mr. Grymer and his friends have a habit of meeting at a pub near the British Museum. A few nights ago, something happened that scared Mangan half to death, worse than running into a madman. Apparently, Mr. Grymer was talking about interesting topics like bodies crawling out of graves when a tall, strange-looking man walked in and started rambling about how they, the brothers, had the ability to escape from the grave and float like straw in the air."
[67] Hardy grumbled, "That's it? What's the big deal? Some people are just naturally timid—"
[68] "That's the problem, " Dr. Phil frowned, "he's not that kind of person. I know Grymer well. If you knew Grymer, you'd understand how strange this is. Hmm, well, go on, kid, what happened next?"
[69] "Mr. Grymer didn't reveal anything. In fact, he joked about it, and the incident ended with a whimper. As soon as the stranger left, a street musician leaning against the pub door played 'The Swing of the Young Rebel in the Sky', and everyone laughed and came back to their senses. Mr. Grymer smiled and said, 'Well, gentlemen, it seems that the dead coming back to life must be more agile, otherwise how could they flutter down from my study window.'"
[70] "The gathering ended. But Mangan couldn't resist his curiosity, so he started investigating the stranger who claimed to be 'Pierre Frey'. Frey left a business card with the name of a theater, so the next day Mangan went to investigate as a journalist. He found that the theater is located in the East End of London, but it's a rundown music hall where various plays are performed every evening. Mangan didn't want to meet Frey, so he first talked to the doorman and was introduced to a special effect artist who performed before Frey. This man called himself 'Pago
[71] "Everyone at the theater called Frey 'the madman' and knew nothing about him. He never talked to anyone and left immediately after each performance. However—the key is, his performance was outstanding. The stunts performer said he couldn't understand why the agents in the West End hadn't noticed such a personage all these years; he could only attribute it to Frey's lack of ambition. His magic was exquisite, especially his disappearing act…"
[72] Hadley grumbles again.
[73] "No," Lambert insists, "As far as I know, Frey's magic is not the old-fashioned kind. Mangan said he performed without an assistant, and all the props were packed into a box the size of a coffin. If you know anything about the magician's trade, you'll realize how incredible that is. In fact, Frey seems to know a lot about coffins and such things. Once 'PagoRiachi Master' asked him why, and he was shocked by the unexpected answer. Frey turned and grinned, 'We had three people buried alive, and only one survived!' PagoRiachi asked, 'How did you escape?' Frey calmly replied, 'I didn't escape, you know, the two who didn't escape included me.'"
[74] Hadley tugs at his earlobe and starts to take it seriously.
[75] "Well," he says uneasily, "It seems more serious than I thought. This guy is crazy, completely crazy. If he really harbors some imagined deep grudge—you said he's a foreigner? I'll call the Home Office and have them keep an eye on him. And if he wants to bother your friend—"
[76] "Has he caused any trouble already?" Dr. Phil asked.
[77] Lambert changed his posture: "Starting from Tuesday, strange letters have been sent to Professor Grierson in every batch of mail. And he silently tears them up each time. But someone told his daughter about the bar incident, and she's very worried. Yesterday, things changed, and Grierson began to show signs of strangeness."
[78] "What happened?" Dr. Phil moved his hand away from his face, his small eyes shining with intensity, staring at Lambert.
[79] "Yesterday, he called Manguin and said, 'Come to my house on Saturday night, someone is threatening me and wants to pay me a visit.' Manguin, of course, suggested he call the police, but Grierson ignored him. Then Manguin said, 'Damn it, Professor, that guy is crazy and probably dangerous. Shouldn't you take some precautions?' The professor replied, 'Oh, yes, I will. I'm going to buy a painting.'"
[80] "What kind of painting?" Hardy sat up straight, asking curiously.
[81] "A painting, one that hangs on the wall. No, I'm not joking. He really bought a landscape painting, with some strange trees, tombstones, and a massive size that required two men to carry it up the stairs. 'Massive' is an exaggeration, I haven't seen it in person yet. The artist is Bernabi, a club member, and also an amateur in criminal studies… well, that's Grimmer's defense measure."
[82] Facing Hardy's suspicious gaze, Rampa repeated himself with some excitement. Then both of them turned to look at Dr. Phil. The doctor sat still, his lips puffing out from under his double chin, his hair a mess, and his hands tightly gripping his cane. He nodded, watching the fire. When he spoke, the comfortable atmosphere in the room diminished.
[83] "Child, do you have Grimmer's address?" His voice was calm and steady, "Good, Hardy, you'd better start the car."
[84] "Okay. But…"
[85] "When a so-called crazy person threatens a normal person," Dr. Phil nodded again, "it's best to ignore it. But when a normal person's behavior starts to act like a crazy person, it makes me quite uneasy. Perhaps nothing will happen tonight, but I have a bad feeling about it." He gasped, struggling to get up, "Come on, Hardy. Let's go check it out. It's like a patrol."
[86] The cold wind blew fiercely on the narrow street near Brother High Street, and the snow had stopped. The streets and gardens along the riverbank were white and dreamlike.
[87] The deserted River Embankment, lit up for performances, was covered in dirty tire tracks. They turned onto Edgware Road and saw a clock display the time as 10:05. Hardy sat quietly in the car, his collar up. Dr. Fell shouted for acceleration when Hardy looked at Lambert and then at the doctor, squeezed in the back seat.
[88] "I think this is all very strange and irrelevant to us," he said grumpily, "and if someone is going to visit him, he's probably already gone."
[89] "I know," Dr. Fell said, "That's exactly what worries me."
[90] The car sped down Southampton Street. Hardy honked the horn, seemingly just to vent his anger - but they were indeed speeding up. The street was desolate, but the next street leading to Russell Square seemed even more desolate. There were a few footprints on the roadside and even fewer tire tracks. Just after passing Kipling Street, if you remembered the phone booth at the northern end, the house opposite would come into view. Lambert saw a simple three-story house with a dark brown painted base and a red brick upper story. Six steps led to a wide front door with a brass border, a mailbox, and a copper handle. The entire house was shrouded in darkness, with only two windows on the first floor illuminated by light, casting light on the small door leading to the basement. This was nothing more than an ordinary house, but the situation was anything but ordinary.
[91] A window blind was torn open and pushed aside, revealing a figure jumping onto the windowsill. The other window was closed, but suddenly one of them slammed open. The figure jumped down, leaping over a row of pointed railings and landing on the sidewalk with a single foot. He slipped on the snow and slid off the curb, almost being run over by a car.
[92] Mr. Hardy slammed on the brakes, bringing the car to a stop on the side of the road. He immediately jumped out and grabbed the man's arm before he could get up. By the headlights, Lambert recognized the man's face.
[93] "Mannion!" he exclaimed. "What's going on—"
[94] Mannion was not wearing a hat or a coat, and his arms and hands were covered in shiny snowflakes. His eyes shone in the light.
[95] "Who?" he croaked. "No, no, I'm fine! Let me go, damn it! Who—Ted! Listen, find someone. You come with me, quick! He locked us in the house—we heard gunshots upstairs. He locked us in the house, you know…"
[96] Lambert looked behind Mangen, seeing the silhouette of a woman at the window. Hardy interrupted Mangen's disjointed narrative.
[97] "Don't worry. Who locked you in the house?"
[98] "It's him, Pierre. He's still inside. We heard gunshots, but the door was too thick to break down. Hey, are you guys coming?"
[99] As he spoke, he rushed up the steps to the front door, Hardy and Lambert following close behind. To their surprise, the front door was unlocked, and Mangen turned the handle and it opened with a click. The entrance was dimly lit, with only a lamp on a distant table. It seemed as if something was watching them from behind, the face more grotesque than imagined Pierre's; then Lambert realized it was just a Japanese samurai armor. Mangen rushed to the right door, turning the key in the lock, the door opened from inside, and the girl who had appeared at the window stood in front of them. Mangen hugged her tightly. Just then, a loud explosion came from upstairs.
[100] "It's okay, Boyd!" Lambert shouted, his heart almost in his throat. "This is Sheriff Hardy—I told you about him. Where is the sound coming from? What is it?"
[101] Manogen pointed to the stairs: "Hurry upstairs, I'll protect Rosette. He's still upstairs, he can't escape. For God's sake, be careful!"
[102] They climbed up the stairs carpeted with thick carpet, and Manogen took down a heavy weapon from the wall. The second floor was pitch black and seemed devoid of life. However, a beam of light shone down from the alcove leading to the next floor, and the sound of the explosion had now turned into a series of heavy blows.
[103] "Professor Grimmer!" a voice cried out, "Professor Grimmer! Can you answer me, please?"
[104] Lanbo didn't have time to ponder why the place was filled with a foreign atmosphere, but he followed Hardy up the second flight of stairs, passing through an open archway, and entering a spacious corridor that spanned the length of the house. The oak paneling stretched all the way to the ceiling, with three curtained windows facing the staircase; the thick black carpet could absorb any footstep; and there were two doors on the short sides of the rectangular space, one on the left side, slightly farther away, open, and the other on the right side, only about ten feet away from the staircase, closed, with a man pounding on it.
[105] As they approached, the man turned around abruptly, although there was no illumination in the hall itself, everything could be seen clearly thanks to the light from the alcove – the source of light was the belly of the bronze Buddha statue in the alcove. A short, panting man was waving his hands wildly. His head was large, topped with a wild mane of hair, and he peered at them through large, framed glasses.
[106] "Boyd?" he called, "or De Riemann? Is it you? Who is it?"
[107] "Police," Hadley said as he strode forward, and the man jumped back.
[108] "You won't get in," the short man said, twisting his hands and making them snap. "But we have to get in. The door is locked from the inside. Someone is locked in the house with Griemore. Someone fired a gun - he didn't answer me. Where is Mrs. Dumont? Bring Mrs. Dumont here! He's still inside, I tell you!"
[109] Hadley turned and snapped at him angrily:
[110] "Stop jumping around and get me a pair of pliers. The key is in the lock, so we can only try to turn it from the outside. I need a pair of pliers, do you have any?"
[111] "I – I don't know where it is –"
[112] Hardy looked at Lanyon.
[113] "Hurry down to my car and look in the toolbox under the back seat. Find the smallest pair of pliers you can, and bring two large screwdrivers with you. He might have a weapon –"
[114] Lanyon turned around and saw Dr. Philips panting as he walked through the archway. The doctor didn't say a word, but his face had lost its rosy glow. Lanyon ran downstairs to find the pliers, and the time it took to do so felt like hours. When he returned, he heard Mangan's voice coming from the room downstairs, along with the girl's hysterical screams.
[115] Hardy remained calm, gently inserting the pliers into the lock and applying a strong grip. He then began to turn them to the left.
[116] "There's something moving inside—" the short man said.
[117] "Okay," Hardy said, "step back!"
[118] He put on a pair of gloves, fully alert, and then suddenly pushed open the door. The door flew open, hitting the wall with a bang, causing the chandelier to shake. Nothing came out, but it seemed like something was trying to come out. Otherwise, the bright room was empty. Lamb looked down and saw something lying in a pool of blood, struggling to crawl on the black carpet with its hands and feet, before choking and collapsing to one side, motionless.

[121] Chapter Three: The Masquerade
[122] "You two stay out here," Hedley hurriedly instructed, "don't let the nervous ones come in."
[123] Dr. Philips dragged his bulky body inside, while Lambert stood guard at the door, arms spread wide to block it. The professor's bulk made it difficult for Hedley to turn him over. Despite gritting his teeth, the professor bled profusely as he struggled towards the door, but it was not from internal bleeding. Hedley propped him up with one knee. [138] The room was about fifteen feet square, with oak paneling on all walls and a thick black carpet on the floor. There was a window on the left wall (from the perspective of the door), with brown velvet curtains fluttering in the wind; bookshelves stood on both sides of the window, with some marble busts on top. A heavy wooden desk with claw feet was placed at a slight distance from the window to make use of the light on the left side of the room. A soft chair was pushed under the desk; a mosaic glass lamp and a bronze ashtray were on the leftmost corner of the desk, with a cigar stub still smoldering in the ashtray. There was a small leather-bound book on the desk, with clean blotting paper underneath; a strange yellow jade bull statue was pressing down on a plate with a few pens and a stack of note pads.

There were bloodstains on the sofa.

[144] It may not be that simple, Lambert thought. There was a sense of impact in the painting, as if the painter had captured the moment when the twisted branches were whipped by the wind in a fit of rage, conveying a sense of desolation and terror. The main color of the painting was gloomy and dark, with a lot of greenish tints on the gray and black backgrounds, and the low mountains in the background were painted white. In the foreground of the painting, through the distorted and tangled branches, one could see three gravestones lined up. The atmosphere was similar to the room, with subtle foreign flavors that were difficult to grasp. The gravestones were gradually disintegrating, and it was hard to tell whether they were about to burst. The two slashes seemed to have no effect on everything in the painting.

[224] She stood at the door, scanning the crowd with her gaze. Unaccountably, a thought arose in Lampedusa's mind: this woman was not simple. She was not strikingly beautiful, but her eyes were dark and shrewd, radiating intelligence and vitality; even though they were now swollen and dry, filled with pain, there was not a single tear. Her appearance was not well-coordinated: she was short, stout, with a broad face and high cheekbones, and her skin had a luster. Lampedusa suddenly had an odd idea: if she took the time to dress up, she would undoubtedly be beautiful. Her dark brown hair was loosely gathered behind her ears, and she wore a simple dark dress with white edges only on her chest; but she did not look shabby at all.

[266] During this questioning, Lambert kept a watchful eye on Dr. Phil, who was pacing about the room, tapping his cane on the carpet and making a constant clatter. He would stoop to examine objects on the floor, peering at them until his glasses slipped down his nose. He scrutinized the paintings, perused the books, and admired the jade water buffalo on the table. He also knelt down to examine the fireplace, then stood up to study the heraldry on the shield. He seemed particularly interested in the shield—and Lambert noticed that Dr. Phil kept glancing at Mrs. Dumont, as if he were drawn to her. Mrs. Dumont's bright eyes held something quite sinister, and her eyes would dart around the room every time Dr. Phil looked at an object. This woman must know something. Her hands were clenched in her lap, trying not to look at Dr. Phil but unable to look away. An invisible battle seemed to be taking place between the two of them.
[267] "There are other questions, Mr. Mills," said Hardcastle. "Especially about the incident at the Wigmore bar and the painting. But those can wait until we have a clearer picture... Can you please ask Miss Grimmer and Mr. Mungen to come up? If Mr. DeRiviere has returned, please ask him to come as well... Thank you. Wait—oh, Phil, do you have any questions?"
[268] Dr. Phil shook his head kindly. Lambert noticed that Mrs. Dumont's knuckles were white.
[269] "Must your friend keep pacing around like that?" she suddenly exclaimed, her voice sharp and her "w" sounds turning into "v" sounds, "It's driving me mad. It's—"
[270] Hardcastle looked at her. "I understand, Mrs. Dumont. I'm sorry, but that's just the way he is."
[271] "Speaking of which, who are you?" she exclaimed, "Barge in on my home like this –"
[272] "Allow me to explain," said Hardy. "I am the Chief Inspector of the Scotland Yard Criminal Investigation Department. This is Mr. Lambert. And this, perhaps you have heard of, is Dr. Gideon Phil."
[273] "Yes, yes, I've heard of him," she nodded vigorously and slammed her hand on the table beside her. "Well, well, well! Even so, can you not be more polite? Can you not at least light a fire to warm the room? It's freezing –"
[274] "I don't agree," said Dr. Phil. "Remember, we must first determine what was burnt in the fireplace. The fire must have been very intense."
[275] Eunice Dumont replied wearily, "Oh, how could you be so foolish? What are you still sitting here for? Everyone knows who the murderer is. It's Freneau, isn't it? You know that, don't you? Don't you? Don't you?"
[276] She looked like a gypsy woman with a vacant face, her eyes burning with anger, as if she had seen Fred hanging on the gallows.
[277] "Do you know Fred?" asked Hardy suddenly.
[278] "No, no, I've never seen him! I mean, I didn't know him until this incident. But Charles told me a bit about him."
[279] "What did he tell you?"
[280] "Ah, well! Fred is a madman. Charles didn't know him, but this guy got it into his head that Charles was making fun of his supernatural magic tricks. He has a brother—" She gestured. "Same sort of thing, you understand? Charles told me that the guy might come by tonight at nine-thirty, and if he did, I had to let him in. But at nine-thirty, when I went to collect the coffee dishes, Charles said, 'If the guy doesn't show up tonight, he probably won't come.' Charles said, 'A man with a grudge is always quick to act.' Alas, he was wrong. At nine-forty-five, the doorbell rang, and I answered it, and there was a man on the steps, handing me a card saying, 'Could you please give this to Professor Grierson and ask if he would like to see me?'"
[281] Hardy leaned over the edge of the leather sofa, observing her closely.
[282] "What about his mask, Mrs.? Don't you find it a bit strange?"
[283] "I didn't see his mask! Didn't you notice that there was only one light in the hall downstairs? Oh! There was another street light behind him, and I could only see his silhouette. He spoke politely and held a business card, and I didn't realize –"
[284] "Wait a moment, if you hear his voice again, can you still recognize it?"
[285] She shrugged, as if shedding a burden from her shoulders.
[286] "No problem! I don't know... yes, of course! But you know, his voice didn't sound right; now that I think about it, it sounded muffled behind the mask. Ah, why do men always –" She leaned back in her chair, suddenly teary-eyed for no apparent reason, "I never would have thought of this! I'm telling the truth, I swear! If someone hurt you, fine, you lay low and wait for the right moment to strike them down. Then your friends go to court and swear on a stack of Bibles that you were somewhere else at the time. You don't have to be like Guy Fawkes' friend, Deryn, wearing greasepaint and playing with children[1]; nor do you have to be like this terrible man, handing over a business card and then going upstairs to kill someone and disappearing out the window. It's like something out of a childhood legend…" Her haughty demeanor suddenly collapsed, and she fell into a fit of hysteria, "Oh, God, Charles! My poor Charles!"
[287] Hardy watched impassively as she regained her composure, just as the distorted, dark painting on the opposite wall seemed to regain its strange, mysterious quality. The emotional storm passed, and although she breathed heavily, her nerves were calm, and her alertness increased. Everyone heard the sound of her nails scraping against the armrest of the chair.
[288] "The man said," Hardy pressed on, "‘Could you please pass on a message to Professor Grierson, asking if he would be willing to meet?’ Well, as we know, Miss Grierson and Mr. Mangan were in the living room downstairs, right?"
[289] She looked at Hardy with a strange expression.
[290] "That's a strange question. I don't know what you're getting at. Yes, I think they were in the living room. I didn't notice."
[291] "Do you remember if the door to the living room was open or closed?"
[292] "I don't know. I suppose it was closed, otherwise the light in the hall would have been brighter."
[293] "Please continue."
[294] "Well, after taking the man's business card, I was about to say, 'Come in, I'll go and announce it,' when I suddenly realized that I couldn't face him alone - a madman! I wanted to run upstairs and get Charles to come down, so I said, 'Please wait here, I'll go and announce it.' Then I slammed the door in his face, locked it with the spring lock, so he couldn't come in. Then I went back to the light and looked at the business card. It's still here with me, I haven't given it to Charles yet. It's blank."
[295] "Blank?"
[296] "There's no text or pattern. I wanted to take it upstairs to Charles and ask him to come down immediately. But poor Miles told you what happened next. I was about to knock on the door when I heard someone walking up the stairs behind me. I turned around, and there he was, tall and thin, walking towards me. But I swear, I locked the door downstairs! I'm not afraid of him! Not at all! I asked him what he was doing upstairs."
[297] "But I couldn't see his mask because the light on the stairs was bright, illuminating the hallway to the end and Charles's door, with him facing away from the light. He answered in French, 'Madame, that little trick won't stop me.' He lowered his collar and stuffed his hat in his pocket. I thought he wouldn't dare face Charles, so I opened the door, and Charles opened it from inside at the same time. That's when I saw the mask, a crimson color like fresh blood. Before I could react, he suddenly rushed into the room, kicked the door shut, turned the key, and locked it from the inside."
[298] She stopped, as if the most thrilling part of the story had been told, and could take a breath.
[299] "Then what?"
[300] She replied woodenly, "As Charles instructed, I walked away, neither making a fuss nor making a scene. But I didn't go far, just down a few steps, still able to see the door, just like poor Stewart. It was - too terrifying. You know, I'm not a young girl, I was still there when the gunshot rang out, when Stewart burst out and hit the door, and even when you all rushed upstairs. But I couldn't hold on any longer, I knew what was happening, and I felt dizzy. I managed to get back to my room on the first floor near the stairs before I passed out. Women can be weak sometimes.” She shuddered, her pale lips trembling, and forced a smile onto her smooth face. "But Stewart was right, no one left that room. God bless us, we had no lies. Whatever that monster did to escape the room, he didn't leave through the door... Now, please, can you let me go see Charles at the hospital?"

[302] * *
[303] [1] On November 5, 1605, a group of Catholic conspirators, including Guy Fawkes, attempted to blow up the British Parliament and assassinate the king in order to restore Catholicism in England. The plot failed, and November 5th became a commemorative day, with people burning effigies of Guy Fawkes and dolls as entertainment. This custom spread to Britain's overseas colonies.
[304] Chapter 5: The Enigmatic Legacy
[305] Dr. Phil spoke up. He stood with his back to the fireplace, dressed in black, his tall figure surrounded by swords and shields, with bookshelves and white statues on either side, reminiscent of a baron from the feudal era, yet not as terrifying as the Baron de Bois-Guilbert. He bit off the end of a cigar, turned his head, and skillfully spat it into the fireplace, sliding his glasses up onto his nose.
[306] "Madam," he turned again, emitting a snort from his nose, sounding the charge of battle, "I won't keep you long. Frankly, I have no doubt about your testimony, nor that of Mr. Mills. [307] Mrs. Dumont's sharp gaze fixed on him, her guard up. It was clear she had heard of Dr. Phil's reputation.

[309] "Very important, Madam. Otherwise, this 'impossible crime' would lose half its weight... You're right, yes. Remember, the snow stopped around 9:30. Right, Mr. Hardy?"

[322] She had been staring down at her clasped hands, her fingers turning inside out and back again, and now she raised her head, her eyes brimming with emotion yet resolute, as if she were still weighing whether to reveal how much she dared. Rosette Grimmer is my daughter. She was born here and there's a record of it. But she doesn't know—no one knows. Please, can you keep this secret for me?"
[324] Slowly, her gaze seemed to change, though she didn't raise her voice. She sounded very eager.

[326] "Really?"

She's probably known all along. Don't underestimate children. They just don't want to say so. That's why the world is such a mess, always pretending that twenty-year-olds have no heart and that forty-year-olds are walking corpses. Pish! Anyway, I was wondering, where did you first meet Mr. Grier? Before you came to England?"

[329] "Yes, in Paris."

[400] "Are you saying he climbed up the chimney?"
[401] "I'm absolutely certain that's not the case. The chimney's interior is too narrow to fit a fist inside. Think about it. He knew he was dying, the police were on their way, and he had a lot of things he had to destroy – a task that was even more important than catching the person who shot him or saving his own life. He was struggling to burn these pieces of evidence in front of the fireplace, which is why the sofa was overturned, the rug was dragged, and there was blood everywhere – do you understand?"
[406] The bright yet chilly hallway was eerily silent.
[407] "What about Mrs. Dumont?" asked Hardly gravely.
[408] "She knew about it. It was their secret. And she loved Grymer."
[409] "If your assumptions are correct, the things he destroyed must have been very important," said Hardly, his eyes widening. "What the devil, how do you know all this? And what secret are they hiding?"
[410] Dr. Phil rubbed his temples and ran a hand through his disheveled hair, assuming an argumentative stance.
[411] "Although there are still many unsolvable mysteries," he said, "it's no harm to tell you a little. Consider, Greymarsh and Dumont are not as French as I am. A woman with high cheekbones, a woman who pronounces 'honest' with an unnecessary 'h', cannot have Latin blood. But that's not important. They are both Hungarian. To be precise, Greymarsh is of Hungarian descent, born as Charles Greymarsh Howard or Carolus Greymarsh Howard. His mother was likely French. He comes from Transylvania, a region that was once part of the Hungarian Kingdom and was later annexed by Romania. In the late 19th century or early 20th century, Charles Greymarsh Howard and his two brothers were imprisoned. Have I told you he had two brothers? One we haven't seen yet, and the other now calls himself Pierre Frey."
[412] "I don't know what crime the Howard brothers committed, but they were sent to the Château d'If prison, near Tréggi in the Carpathian Mountains, to dig salt mines. Charles must have escaped. But his life-threatening 'secret' cannot be related to his imprisonment or even his escape before serving his full sentence. Since the Hungarian Kingdom no longer exists, the power has ceased to exist. So it's more likely that he committed atrocities against his two brothers, involving those three coffins and the act of burying people alive; even if it happened years ago, once revealed, he would be hanged… That's my current conclusion. Who has a match?"

[419] Chapter 6: The Seven Towers
[420] Dr. Hardly remained silent for a while, tossing the matchbox at the doctor and glaring at him.

[433] "That may be true, but imagine a learned Frenchman wanting to read English works. [434] "If his surname is Howard, why did he hide his identity for years? Upon hearing 'buried alive' and 'salt mine', I suddenly had an idea. Just as I was thinking this, the remarkable Miles mentioned the man named Frey in the bar. Miles said that although he had never seen Frey, he felt a strange familiarity about him; and Frey's tone of speech was imitating Grimmer. In fact, Frey was implying Grimmer, wasn't he? Brothers, brothers, brothers! Think about it, three coffins, but Frey only mentioned two brothers. It sounded like he was the third brother."
[435] "I was pondering this when a typical Slavic-looking Mrs. Dumont entered. If we can confirm that Grimmer comes from Transylvania, we can narrow down the scope when investigating his background. [436] "I can't think of Transylvania," the inspector roared. "It's more like the American West, 'Buffalo Bill', and Indians. Wait! So you asked Dumont if Grymo had been to America?"
[437] Dr. Philips nodded guiltily. "This question seemed safe, so she answered. I asked Madame Ernes about this, and the answer was as expected. Then I threw the grenade. If Grymo had nothing to do with the salt mines, this question would have no impact. Now you understand what I mean by 'Seven Towers' and 'non-existent country'. God, can anyone give me a match?"

[818] Last night, a man was found dead in front of WC1 on Chalmer Street. [822] In their line of sight, the entire street was deserted from end to end. The weapon was a long-barreled Colt .38 revolver, an old model from thirty years ago, lying ten feet away from the body.
[823] Although all the witnesses heard the statement and the gun was some distance away from the body, they concluded that the victim had committed suicide due to the lack of people on the street. [824] The most shocking aspect surfaced during the autopsy. The wounds on the victim's coat had singed edges, indicating that the gun muzzle must have been pressed firmly against the victim's back, or at most a few inches away. Dr. Jenkins concluded – later supported by the police – that suicide was impossible. He emphasized that no one could shoot themselves from this angle in the back, let alone using a long-barreled gun. This was a murder, but an incredible one. If the distance had been greater, such as from a window or behind a door, it would not be surprising for the killer to have disappeared without leaving a trace. However, the one who killed him was someone who was up close, had a conversation, and then vanished into thin air.

[880] "Ghosts are evil, and the more evil, the better," Dr. Philips boomed, making a face and glaring menacingly. "I don't need lullabies by the bedside or sweet nothings from the Garden of Eden. I want blood! And that's not all. I've got a few rules for you, Mr. Petit. Ghosts must be evil and never speak. They must never be transparent but should be a tangible entity. They shouldn't take up too much space in the story, but each time they appear, it should be with great effect, such as a face suddenly popping out from around a corner. They must not appear in broad daylight, but only in places steeped in history, such as libraries or monasteries. There's a bad trend nowadays, where people scoff at old libraries and ancient ruins and advocate that real ghosts should appear in places like candy stores or lemonade stands. They even call it 'contemporary experimentalism.' Well, that's a fine idea, a real-life experiment. But the fact is, people only get scared in old ruins and graveyards. Unless someone really sees something in a lemonade stand and screams or faints, it's just a load of rubbish."
[881] "Perhaps someone might say," Pettigrew raised one eyebrow, "that the ruins and wrecks are nothing but rubbish. Do you really think contemporary authors cannot write thrilling ghost stories?"
[882] "Of course, it can be written, and more brilliant authors will emerge - as long as they are willing. The problem is, they harbor a fear of what is called popular drama. So, if they cannot erase the essence of popular drama, they resort to using convoluted and confusing techniques to obscure their intentions, resulting in no one understanding what they are trying to convey. They refuse to directly state what the characters see and hear, instead obsessively creating what they call 'impressions.' For example, imagine a butler at a ball announcing the arriving guests, flinging open the drawing room doors and shouting, 'The gleaming top hats dazzle my eyes, or perhaps I misread the reflection from the umbrella stand?' His master would surely be displeased, as he is only concerned with who the guests are. If a story is analyzed like solving an algebra problem, it would no longer be considered terrifying. Imagine someone listening to a joke on Saturday night and laughing aloud only on Sunday morning at church. How sad! But even sadder is someone reading a horror story on Saturday night and only two weeks later suddenly snapping their fingers, realizing they should have been terrified at the time. In my opinion -"

This visitor, however, imitated me too well, which almost seemed to exonerate me. Whether you prefer a more direct or roundabout explanation, I can prove my innocence – either I'm not clever enough, or I'm too clever for my own good. Either way, it holds true."

[958] Chapter 13: The Mysterious Apartment
[959] It was a cold winter day in London, and on a Sunday morning, the city was shrouded in a dull, gray haze. The streets and alleys for miles around were deserted and eerie, and the street where Hardy's car was about to enter, Cardigan Street, was like a sleeping domain.
[960] Dr. Phil was right; Cardigan Street was lined with dirty little shops and rented apartments, located near Lamcondi Street. It was a secluded area. Lamcondi Street itself was a shopping center for the surrounding area, extending north to the quiet and humble Gilford Street and south to the busy thoroughfare of Cheapside Road. At the end of Gilford Street, on the west side, was the entrance to Cardigan Street, with a stationery store and a butcher's shop on either side. From the entrance, the street looked like a narrow alley, and it was easy to miss if one didn't pay attention to the sign. But as soon as one passed the two shops, the view suddenly opened up, revealing a street that was surprisingly wide and stretched for over two hundred yards to the end, where a vacant brick wall stood.
[961] Every time he wandered through the streets of London, a sense of eerie and macabre atmosphere, or the surreal and illusory aura of the rows of buildings, lingered in Lombard's mind. It was like you would wonder if the street would suddenly change its appearance in the night? The faces smiling at you from the doorway might be strangers? He stood with Dr. Phil and Hardy at the street corner, staring straight ahead, where the cramped shops occupied only a small stretch on either side, all with their shutters drawn or a closed, ornate iron frame outside the windows, like fortresses that kept customers at bay. Even the gilded signs exuded a sense of disdain. Every window was impeccably clean, from the dazzling jewelry store at the far right to the gloomy tobacco shop at the nearest left. The merchandise in the tobacco shop was withered and shrunken, looking even worse than dried tobacco leaves; it was tucked away in a corner, half-hidden behind a poster board; and the posters on the board were of news from an unknown year.
[962] "Pull yourself together!" Dr. Phil grumbled as he stumbled forward, his heavy footsteps echoing, "Let's get moving before we attract attention. Where was Fred when he was attacked? Show me."
[963] Hardy pointed to the tobacco shop nearby.

[966] "Alright, it is nonsense, but are there any other possibilities? You said it yourself, the street is so long and clear, there's no way to pull a trick! I know there's no such trick, but what happened? None of the witnesses saw anything; and if there was something, they would have seen it. Stand still and keep your direction. I'm going a few steps ahead, then I'll turn around—like this. Now, how far am I from you?" [969] "This distance is quite close," Dr. Philips pushed his wide-brimmed hat back, "The two of them were less than 30 feet away from Fred when they heard the gunshot, and immediately turned around—uh—uh…"

There's a jewelry store window not far away to your right rear. Can you see it? Good. There's a light on in the window, not very bright, but better than nothing. Now, explain to me, could the two witnesses have missed seeing someone approaching Fred while standing where I am now?"
[972] He raised his voice, and the echo of derision echoed on the street. The discarded newspaper was swept up by the whirlwind of the cold wind and struggled to escape; the wind howled into the chimney, like a piercing scream as it passed through a tunnel. Dr. Phil's black cloak fluttered in the wind, and the black ribbon on his glasses danced wildly.

Dr. Phil stared at the moving eyes; they witnessed a murder and still watched the crime scene with a smile, undoubtedly adding a layer of horror to Kahlil Street. Dr. Phil swayed back to the middle of the street.

[1030] Chapter 14: The Clue of the Church Bell

[1306] Chapter 17: The Lecture on the Study of the Room
[1307] The coffee was served, the bottles were empty, and the smoke of the cigars rose gently. In the dimly lit restaurant of the hotel where Petty stayed, Hardy, Petty, Lamb, and Dr. Phil sat around the halo of the red lamp. The other guests at the tables were few, but they still lingered, enjoying the warm fire, watching the snowflakes falling gently outside, and basking in the leisurely and comfortable atmosphere of the winter afternoon. In the presence of armor and shield emblems, Dr. Phil looked like a proud feudal lord, contemptuously surveying the coffee cup, as if he were about to swallow it whole. He casually raised his cigar with a swagger, cleared his throat.
[1308] "Next, I will discuss the general methods for constructing a 'sealed room' in the mystery novel and its historical evolution process," he said, his tone neither too harsh nor too gentle, but very assertive.
[1309] "Let's talk about it later," Hardy groaned at the mention of it. "After a hearty meal, who would still have the mood to listen to a lecture? Besides, there's something to do. Hey, I was just saying -"
[1310] Dr. Phil ignored Hardy and continued, "Next, I will discuss the general methods for constructing a 'sealed room' in the mystery novel and its historical evolution process." He cleared his throat. "Hmm, if you have any objections, please ignore them. Hmm, everyone! First of all, after reviewing the countless masterpieces of the past forty years, my perspective has broadened, and I can say -"
[1311]"Since we are analyzing impossible crimes," Peetis interrupted, "why switch to detective novels?"
[1312]"Because we are in a detective novel," Dr. Philip replied calmly. "Why deceive ourselves and deceive readers? Discussing detective novels doesn't require any pretentious excuses. Enjoy the highest pleasure of sailing through the sea of books and enjoying the thrill of hunting down the killer."
[1313]Speaking of the main point, everyone, to avoid controversy, I will not prescribe any rules during the discussion, but will base everything on personal taste and preference. Let me rephrase Kipling's famous saying: "There are sixty-nine ways to build a murder maze, and any one of them is right." However, if I claim that all methods are equally interesting to me, then I must be – to put it mildly – a liar. But that's not the point. Some people say that the interest of the closed room theme in detective novels is unparalleled, which is unduly biased. I like bloodthirsty, bizarre, and grotesque killers, and I also like vivid and imaginative plot structures, but so far I have not found a novel that is purely based on something that is rarely heard of and almost impossible to happen, and makes people feel intoxicated. How can the old clichés compare to the low whisper of Detective Hanard and the death knell of St. Paul's Church? I admit that this is my self-entertaining but rational personal bias, and it is not worth mentioning. It doesn't matter if others point it out.
[1314]"But this viewpoint has its own necessity, because some people who cannot tolerate even the slightest degree of terror insist on elevating their preferences to a golden rule. The phrase 'absolutely impossible' has become a label-style difficulty tool in their hands. Naive readers are bound to fall into the trap and equate 'absolutely impossible' with 'pathetic and mediocre.'"
[1315]"The phrase 'absolutely impossible' is not applicable to the criticism of detective novels. My judgment is based on reason and evidence, and readers' love for detective novels is largely dependent on what is called the impossible crime. A is murdered, B and C have major suspicions, D who looks innocent is not the killer, but the truth is that D is the killer. All witnesses claim that G has a perfect alibi and has no chance to commit the crime, but it turns out that G is the one who did it. Detective picks up a few bits of coal ash on the beach, such a trivial thing is not likely to be an important clue, but in the end, it becomes the key to solving the mystery. In short, as the case progresses, 'absolutely impossible' gradually becomes a laughingstock. It is not until the end of the story that one should not speak lightly of 'absolutely impossible.' Therefore, one must bet on the possibility of the suspect with the lowest possibility (as is the case with the conservative) in order not to be full of complaints at the end, because compared to the other suspects, this gentleman appears to lack the motive and necessity of the crime, and is the least conspicuous in the entire story."<|
[1316] "When readers shout 'it won't happen,' when you get angry at the half-faced demon, the ghost with a veil, and the beautiful seductive siren, you are essentially saying, 'I don't like this story.' It's okay, if you don't like it, you have the right to say it out loud. But if you directly convert personal taste into a criterion for evaluating the quality and credibility of a work, you are essentially saying, 'This series of events is impossible, otherwise it would be too boring.'"
[1317] "What is the truth? Since the locked-room genre is criticized the most and is considered the least credible, let's take it as an example and conduct a thorough analysis."
[1318] "It is reassuring that most people like locked-room mysteries. However—the fatal obstacle—even the most loyal fans of locked-room mysteries are bound to have doubts. So, we are on the same side, and we can look forward to whether this discussion can make any discoveries. Why do we always have doubts when uncovering the mystery of the locked room? This has nothing to do with paranoia, but only because we feel a certain sense of disappointment. Disappointment leads to unfairly degrading the entire story as unbelievable, impossible, and absurd. It is only natural."
[1319] "To put it simply," Dr. Phil raised his cigar and said loudly, "Olock's magic trick today has been successfully replicated in reality. Goodness, everyone, we sneer at real-life cases, so what will be the fate of fictional novels? Every time this happens, the magician's trick becomes even more impenetrable. In detective novels, we call it 'impossible crimes'; in reality, even though we have to believe that it really exists, we cannot help but feel disappointed. The two feelings of disappointment have the same cause—too high expectations."
[1320] "Since the puzzle is so magical,
[1321] "Enough, enough," Hardy adjusted his posture, "I don't have much to say about this topic. But since you insist on giving a long speech, it must be related to this case in some way…"
[1322] "That's right."
[1323] "Then why do you use the locked-room example? You've said yourself that the death of Germer is not the biggest mystery, the biggest mystery is still the gunshot in the middle of the deserted street…"
[1324] "Oh, that one? Dr. Phil casually waved his hand, making Hardy's eyes widen, "That problem? As soon as I hear the church bells, the answer comes to me. Oh, the marvelous hidden message! I'm serious. Now, it's the mystery of escaping from the locked room that really confuses me. To untangle the mess and unravel the threads, I will summarize the different methods of locked-room murder in a brief classification. It's undeniable that this case must belong to one of these types! No matter how the locked-room trick changes, it is only a modified and mutated version of a few core methods."
[1325] "Hmmph! Ha! Assuming this locked room has a door, a window, and thick walls. Taking the premise of locking all doors and windows as the basis for discussing escape methods, I first exclude the 'secret passage' type of 'trick' that is lowly and rare nowadays. This kind of solution is completely unacceptable, and even if the author has a sense of self-respect, they don't even need to explicitly state that there is no secret passage. Various small tricks that have a borderline touch are also not included in the discussion, such as: a gap in the wall that only allows one hand to pass through; a blade thrown into the ceiling's bolt hole, with the bolt hole then being sealed with a plug that cannot be noticed by anyone, and the upper floor's floorboards being dusted to create the illusion that no one has walked over. Although small, the nature of the violation is no different
[1326] "Makes sense," said Petty with a smile. "Continue."
[1327] "First! The locked room in the crime scene is truly airtight, and the reason the killer didn't escape is that the killer was never actually in the locked room. The answer is as follows:
[1328]
[1329] "1. This case is not a murder but an accident caused by a series of coincidences and mistakes that make it seem like a murder. Before the room was locked, there was a robbery or a fight, someone was injured, furniture was damaged, which could make people think of a scene of a fierce struggle when killing. Later, the victim either died accidentally or fainted in the locked room, but these events were mistakenly believed to have occurred at the same time. In such cases, the cause of death is often skull fracture - caused by hitting a piece of furniture, such as a table corner, the protruding edge of a chair, but the most famous culprit is the iron fireplace mantel. Notably, since Sherlock Holmes' 'The Hunchback', the deadly mantel has been stained with blood. The most perfect solution (including one murderer) in this type of situation is Gaston Leroux's 'The Secret of the Yellow Room' - one of the greatest detective novels in history.
[1330] "2. This case is indeed a murder, but the victim falls into a trap and kills himself or accidentally walks into a death trap. The methods may use the eerie atmosphere of a haunted house, or psychological suggestion, or more commonly, poison gas from outside the house. Poison gas or poison makes the victim go crazy, smashing the room into chaos, making it seem like a fight had taken place, and the victim accidentally stabs himself with a knife. Variations of this method include: the victim's head being pierced by the sharp points of a chandelier; being hanged with an iron coil; even being strangled to death by their own hands.
[1331] "So," Dr. Phil tapped his cigar, "the study of these mechanical devices led us from the narrow confines of the locked room to the broader field of 'impossible crimes.' These methods could potentially evolve further, even to the point of electrocution. The ropes in front of the paintings could be electrified, as could the chessboard, and even the gloves. Every piece of furniture, including the teapot, conceals a potential danger. However, these methods have not yet been used, so next is:
[1332] "4. The case was suicide, but deliberately disguised as murder. The victim killed himself with an ice pick, and after the ice pick melted, the weapon could not be found in the sealed room, leading to a ruling of murder. Or the victim committed suicide by gunshot, with a rubber band tied to the gun's tail. When the victim let go, the weapon was pulled into the chimney and hidden. Variations of this trick (without creating a sealed room) include: a weight tied to a string on the gun, which is pulled over a bridge railing and falls into the water after the gunshot; similarly, the gun can be pulled out of a window and fall into a snowbank."
[1333] "5. The case is a murder, and the killer uses magic tricks and disguises to create confusion. For example, there is someone monitoring the door, and everyone thinks the victim is still safe, but in fact, the victim has already been killed inside the room. The killer disguises himself as the victim (or his silhouette is mistaken for the victim) and quickly enters the room, immediately turns around to remove the disguise, and quickly leaves the room with his true appearance, creating the illusion that he passed by someone when he left. An alibi is established, as the time of the murder is assumed to be after the fake 'victim' entered the room."
[1334] "6. The case is indeed a murder, and the killer acted outside the room to create the illusion that the killer must have been inside the room at the time of the incident."
[1335] "For the sake of simplicity," Dr. Phil paused for a moment, "I will refer to this type of murder as 'remote murder' or 'ice-pick murder' because the underlying principle is the same in all cases. The ice-pick method has already been mentioned, and you are all familiar with it. The door was locked, the windows were too small for the killer to enter, yet the victim was clearly killed inside the room, and the weapon was nowhere to be found. The ice-pick, like the mysterious gas, was fired from outside the house – we won't delve into its feasibility – and melted away without a trace. Anna Katherine Green first introduced this trick in her novel, The Name of the Game.
[1336] "Speaking of tangents, the origins of several classic patterns in mystery novels can be traced back to Mrs. Greene. Over fifty years ago, in her debut mystery novel, she pioneered the trope of the ruthless secretary murdering her employer; and I believe, based on current statistics, the secretary remains the most common killer in novels. The butler has become passé; the wheelchair-bound disabled person is too suspicious; the prim and proper spinster is no longer a serial killer but a detective; doctors have become too predictable, unless they are famous and turn into mad scientists. Lawyers remain as cunning as ever, but only in certain cases do they take the initiative. History has a way of repeating itself! Eighty years ago, Edgar Allan Poe revealed the identity of his killer as 'Goodfellow' [5]; and contemporary bestselling mystery novelists have followed suit, giving their masterminds behind-the-scenes names like 'Goodman' [6]. Regardless, in a mansion, the secretary remains the most dangerous character.
[1337] "Continuing the discussion of the 'Ice Pick Murder' pattern. Its implementation is credited to the famous Medici family during the Renaissance period; a famous Fleming Stone novel series [7] referenced a satirical short poem about war, describing the role of ice picks in the decline of the Roman Empire in the first century. Ice picks could be fired from guns, thrown by hand, or launched with a crossbow, as demonstrated in a case handled by Hamilton Crow (the charming character known as 'The Man of a Thousand Faces') [8]. Variations of this technique could involve dissolvable projectiles, such as bullets made of salt, or even bullets made of frozen blood, and so on.
[1338] "The 'Ice Pick Murder' pattern illustrates my point: while the murder takes place indoors, the killer can be located outside. There are other methods. The murder weapon could be a cane with a hidden blade, striking the victim through the dense foliage of the pergola and then retracting; if the blade is exceptionally thin, the victim may not even realize they have been fatally wounded until they collapse in another room. Alternatively, the killer could lure the victim to look out the window, despite the safety below, and then drop the ice - our old friend - from above, shattering the skull and causing death; the weapon would be impossible to find as it would have melted.
[1339] "Methods of murder using venomous snakes or insects can also be classified in this category (in fact, it would be appropriate to list them in the third category). In addition to closets and safes, flowerpots, stacks of books, chandeliers, and even walking sticks can serve as hiding places for snakes. I recall a vivid example: the victim was about to put a highly exaggerated and bizarrely carved scorpion-shaped pipe in his mouth when the scorpion suddenly came to life and started waving its claws. However, when it comes to the most exciting long-range murder methods in the history of mystery novels, I would like to recommend one of the most thrilling short stories in the genre (there are a few other masterpieces that can be considered equally impressive and can be placed alongside the pinnacle of excellence that later generations struggle to reach, such as Thomas Burke's "The Hand of Mr. Ottemore," G.K. Chesterton's "The Man in the Passage," and Jack Faulkner's "Escape from Cell No. 13"): Melville Davisson Post's "The Dumdorf Mystery" – the culprit of the long-range murder was the sun. On the table in Dumdorf's room was a bottle of unprocessed methanol, and the sunlight streaming through the window of the locked room focused on the bottle like a convex lens, igniting the detonator of the gun hanging on the wall, which caused the obnoxious person lying on the bed to be blown apart. In addition to this, there are –
[1340] "Hold on! Uh-huh. Hmm. I think I'll just stop here. Let me wrap up the classification work with the last type:
[1341] "7. The case is indeed a murder, but the method is exactly the opposite of the fifth type. That is, the presumed time of death of the victim is far earlier than the actual time of the crime. Despite everyone knocking on the door, the victim in the room remained unconscious (although drugged, but not fatal). The killer pretended to be frightened, forcefully broke the door, rushed into the room first, and stabbed (or slit the throat, etc.) the victim in an instant, misleading other eyewitnesses to believe that they saw something that they did not actually see. The honor of inventing this trick belongs to Israel Zangwill [10], and its various variations have flourished for years. The locations where this type of technique (usually involving stabbing) is used include: ships, abandoned houses, greenhouses, attics, and even outdoor locations – the victim collapses and becomes unconscious, and the killer takes advantage of the opportunity to strike while checking on the victim. Therefore –"
[1342] "Hold on! Wait a minute!" Dr. Hardy suddenly pounded the table with a heavy fist. Dr. Phil was in the midst of a passionate speech, smiling and looking at him with amusement. Dr. Hardy continued, "Your analysis is truly impressive, as you seem to have covered all the branches of the mystery in the room –"
[1343] "All?" Dr. Phil widened his eyes and snorted, "Far from it. The analysis above has not yet exhausted all the specific techniques in this particular type, but it is a rough outline of my spontaneous inference, to be further summarized later. Next, I will analyze another major category: various techniques for locking doors and windows from the room. Hmm, haha! So, ladies and gentlemen, let's get to the point -"
[1344] "Don't rush things," the sheriff stubbornly interjected, "Your argument is contradictory. You claim to be able to unravel these cunning devices and find clues, but none of the seven points you just summarized apply to this case.
