[1355] Pettigrew tapped his gold pencil on the notebook and chuckled, "Anyway, you have to wrap up the current topic. The chief's words are not without reason. How to manipulate the door, window, and chimney, please enlighten us."
[1356] "Speaking of chimneys, I'm sorry to say," Dr. Phil refocused, suddenly full of enthusiasm, "it's quite unfortunate that in the realm of detective novels, the chimney's escape function is not well-regarded - of course, as a secret passage, it's a great one. Some chimneys are hollow, with secret rooms behind them; some fireplaces can be drawn back like curtains; some fireplaces can be rotated to one side, creating ample space; and even underneath the fireplace's base, there may be a hidden space. Furthermore, chimneys can be an excellent channel for delivering supplies, particularly poisonous ones. However, it's quite rare for a killer to choose the chimney as an escape route. Not only is its feasibility almost zero, but it's also much more clumsy than manipulating doors and windows. Among the two major channels, door manipulation is more common, and I will list some methods to create the illusion of a locked door from the inside:
[1357] "1. Using the key still inserted in the lock. This classic technique has been used for years, but due to its various variations being well-known, it is rarely effective. One example is using pliers to twist the key in the lock from the outside. Another practical little gadget is a thin metal bar about two inches long, with one end tied to a long and sturdy fine line. Before leaving the room, insert the metal bar into the small hole at the top of the key, one end up and the other down, simulating a lever. The fine line hangs down to the ground, pulled out from under the door. After closing the door from the outside, simply pull the fine line, and the lever turns the key to lock the door. The metal bar can then be detached and pulled out from under the door to recover it. There are also many variations of this principle, but the fine line is a must.
[1358] "2. Removing the door hinges without damaging the door lock or latch. This technique is clean and efficient, and many mischievous boys who want to get something from a locked cabinet are familiar with it. However, the hinges must be installed on the outside.
[1359] "3. Using the latch. The fine line comes into play again, this time with a safety pin and a sewing needle. First, make a lever with a safety pin inside the door, and use the fine line to pull the sewing needle through the lock to pull down the latch from the outside. I pay tribute to Philo Vance, who used this technique to perfection. The fine line has many simpler but less effective uses.
[1360] "4. Making the latch fall and lock automatically. This technique usually requires placing a support under the latch, then closing the door from the outside and removing the support to make the latch fall. The most ideal support so far has been the beloved ice cube: when melted, the ice cube under the latch falls, and the room is sealed. Another case succeeded in knocking down the inner latch just by the force of closing the door.
[1361] "5. Create a simple and effective illusion. The killer locks the door from the outside after committing the crime, hiding the key on their person, while the crowd mistakenly believes the key is still in the lock inside the door. The killer initiates panic, discovers the body, breaks the glass panel on the door, reaches in with the key hidden in their hand and inserts it into the lock inside the door, and then 'discover' the key, opening the door. This technique can also be applied to the situation of breaking down a standard wooden door panel."
[1362] "There are countless similar tricks, such as locking the door from the outside and using a thin string to return the key to the room, etc. But I'm sure you can see that all these tricks are useless in this case. We found that the door was locked from the inside, oh, the murderer had countless ways to do it - but none of them could be accomplished, because Mills was watching the door the whole time. So the process of locking the door was not mysterious, it was completed under the watchful eye of the witness. We are at a dead end."
[1363] "I didn't want to repeat myself," Pettigrew furrowed his brow, "but all the impossible methods have been ruled out, and the remaining options, no matter how incredible, must be the correct answer. You've ruled out the door, so should the chimney also be off the table?"
[1364] "Yes," Dr. Phil murmured.
[1365] "So, back to the windows again?” asked Hardly, "After all that talk, it seems like a waste of time. But in all your impressive categorization, you missed the only possible escape route the killer could have used..."
[1366] "Because the window was not locked," Dr. Phil shouted. "If it had been locked, I could have come up with several clever methods. From the fake nail heads in the past to the recent steel louvers, the principle is the same. You could also break the window, carefully hook the lock, and leave by replacing the glass with a new one with adhesive; since the new glass looks just like the original, it appears that the window was locked from the inside. But in this case, the window was not only unlocked but also open – it just couldn't be climbed."
[1367] "I seem to have read somewhere that someone can fly…" Pettigrew reminded.
[1368] Dr. Phil shook his head vigorously: "Even if someone could fly and walk on a smooth wall, I would welcome the idea of the killer escaping by air. As long as there is a place for it to take off and land, I would believe it. In other words, the killer has to take off from somewhere and land somewhere. But unfortunately, there are no traces of takeoff and landing on the roof and ground…"
[1369] Dr. Phil stopped abruptly, raised his head. In the silence and emptiness of the dining room, the snowflakes on the window glass flickered with a faint glow. A figure rushed in, hesitated for a moment, looked around, and hurried towards them. When they saw Mungen, Hardy couldn't help but whisper in surprise. Mungen's face was pale.
[1370] "Is something wrong again?" Hardy's tone was cold as ice, and he pushed his chair back. "Is it that the coat has changed color again, or –"
[1371] "No, that's not it," Morgen stood by the table, panting, "But you should go over there. Dreyman had an accident. It seems he had a stroke. No, he's not dead yet, but the situation is not good. He was trying to contact you when he had the stroke... He kept saying nonsense, like 'someone in his room', 'fireworks', and 'chimney'."

[1384] Chapter 18: The Chimney
[1385] The three people still waiting in the living room were visibly exhausted and on edge. Even Stewart Mills, standing with his back to the fireplace, was clearing his throat, driving Rosette to the brink of madness. When Mangan led Dr. Phil, Hardy, Peetes, and Lamb into the room, Ernestine Dumont was sitting silently by the fireplace. Bernabee had already left.

The three brothers were sentenced to twenty years of labor for robbing the Banque de Cuneau in Blaise in November 1898. During the robbery, the bank security guard was seriously injured and died, and the whereabouts of the huge amount of stolen money are unknown, it is said to have been hidden elsewhere. In August 1900, when the plague broke out, the three of them escaped with the help of the prison doctor and were buried in the plague area. An hour after burial, the prison guards J. Rene and R. George returned to insert a wooden cross into the grave as a marker, but they found that Carlos Howard's tomb was in an abnormal state, and when they approached to investigate, they saw that the coffin lid was open and the body had disappeared. They dug up the other two tombs and found that Pierre Howard was covered in blood and unconscious, but still alive; Nicolas Howard had suffocated and died. After confirming that Nicolas had no chance of survival, the prison guards buried him again, and Pierre was returned to prison. The prison kept this scandal a secret and did not pursue the fugitives; until the end of the war, the case was never known to outsiders. Since his return, Pierre Frey Howard's mental state has never recovered, and he was released on January 19, 1919. I guarantee you that the youngest brother is no longer alive.

[1476] "I know," Dorothy said proudly. "While you were out running around this afternoon, I did a little research. And I'll bet that even if this method is extremely simple, it won't work. It's a waste of time!"
[1477] "Have you read Gross's works?"
[1478] "Well, I've read the English version. It's quite simple. The theory is that, if you burn a letter and pay attention, the charred parts of the letter will actually appear clearer; usually black on white, or black on gray, sometimes the opposite. Have you ever experienced this?"
[1479] "I'm afraid not. I hardly saw an open fireplace before coming to England. Is this really true?"
[1480] Dorothy frowned. "It works well with charred paper that has writing on it, like a box or a soap box. But with ordinary manuscripts – well, the identification process goes like this: you pin a sheet of transparent tracing paper to a board with thumbtacks, then glue the charred fragment onto the tracing paper, and press hard on the fragment…"
[1481] "How can you press it after it's burned like that? Won't it break?"
[1482] "Ah-ha! That's the trick. You have to soften the paper first. You put the tracing paper into a frame that's a few inches high, put all the paper pieces on it, then lay a wet towel on top that's folded several times, so the paper pieces absorb the moisture and naturally expand. After it's thoroughly expanded and fixed, you cut the tracing paper along the edges of each burnt paper, move it onto a glass plate, and rearrange it like a puzzle. Then, you place a second glass plate on top of the first one, fix the edges, and the writing becomes clear when you shine a light on it. But I dare say –"
[1483] "Why not try it yourself?" Lombard exclaimed, rubbing his hands together.
[1484] The first attempt to burn the paper was unsuccessful. Lombard took an old piece of paper from his pocket, lit a match, and set it on fire. Although he tried to control the speed, the paper was quickly engulfed in flames and curled inward; he let go of it, and it floated to the edge of the fireplace, where it shriveled and blackened like an umbrella, barely two inches long. They knelt on the ground, unable to see any writing from any angle. Lombard burned several more pieces of paper, but they all floated away like beautiful fireworks, disappearing into the ashes of the fireplace. He gradually lost patience and grabbed anything within reach to burn more intensely; the more agitated he became, the more convinced he was that the method would work if used properly. So, he also included typing papers in his experiments, typing several pages of "Dear kind people, it's time to help these people" on Dr. Phil's typewriter; in the end, the carpet was covered in shredded paper.

He suddenly realizes that his time has come, spitting blood from his mouth like a severed artery; he struggles to overturn the sofa, knock over the chairs, and ignite the firecrackers with his last ounce of strength. A lifetime of grudges, escapes, and schemes unfolds before him, gradually fading into a dark and endless night. He wants to scream, but it is too late; his throat is already soaked in blood. At that moment, Charles Grimmer suddenly realizes that he has never truly believed that he could escape the ultimate and most earth-shattering mirror magic of his perilous life…"

[1760] "He knows he is walking towards death," said Dr. Phil, "and surprisingly, he seems to be at peace with it."

The door opened, and a woman's silhouette stood at the door, her expression terrifying. She was dressed in black, but still wore the red and yellow shawl that reminded her of her beloved.

[1765] "Yes. Later, when Grimmer opened the door and stepped out into the glow of the streetlights, panic and terror struck him without warning. Compare this:
[1766] 'He was there one moment, and then he was gone... I'm going to tell you who my brother is, so you don't think I'm talking nonsense...'
[1767] "This statement makes sense. He thought no one knew about Frey's existence. Based on this premise, let's re-examine the jumbled, obscure, and perplexing fragments of words – he heard the doctor declare his own imminent death – he wanted to explain the entire riddle to us.
[1768] "He first wanted to tell us about the Howard brothers and the salt mine, and then jumped to Frey's death and how Frey took him down. 'It wasn't suicide' – he saw Frey on the street, so his plan to disguise Frey's death as a suicide failed. 'He couldn't use a rope' – Greymore had already thrown away the rope, so Frey couldn't have escaped using it from the scene. 'The roof' – Greymore was referring to the roof he had passed when fleeing Frey's apartment, not his own. 'Snow' – once the snow stopped, his plan failed. 'The light was too bright' – this statement is crucial, Hardy! When he looked out onto the street, the streetlights were too bright, and Frey saw him, leading to Frey opening fire. 'He had a gun' – Frey, of course, had a gun at the time. 'Fox' – 'Fox' refers to Guy Fawkes' mask. Lastly, 'Don't blame the poor' – not De Rem, he wasn't referring to De Rem; I believe this was a lie he told in a moment of desperation: 'Don't blame the poor Pete, I didn't mean to involve him.'
